Both simulations were carried out in a periodic box ad- vancing 21603dark matter particles from redshift z‚àº127 to 0. The side length of the box is 761 Mpc for the MS, and 152 Mpc for the MS-II. The corresponding particle masses are 1.58√ó109M‚äôand 1.27√ó107M‚äô, respectively. The large volume ofthe MSenables ustosample reliably eventhe cen- tral galaxies of rar
osample reliably eventhe cen- tral galaxies of rare and massive clusters, while the MS-II has suÔ¨Écient resolution to sample the faintest dwarf galax- ies. Dark matter haloes are identiÔ¨Åed at each output using a friends-of-friends (FoF) halo Ô¨Ånder that links together p ar- ticles with separation less than 0.2 of the mean interpartic le distance. Substructures inside the FoF groups are then iden - tiÔ¨Åed using subfind (Springel et al. 2001). The most mas- sive self-bound subgroup is deÔ¨Åned as the ‚Äò
t mas- sive self-bound subgroup is deÔ¨Åned as the ‚Äòmain halo‚Äô, and all other subgroups within the FoF groups are referred to as ‚Äòsubhaloes‚Äô. The virial radius and mass of the main haloesare determined with a spherical overdensity criterion: the density inside a sphere centered on the most bound particle is required tobe equal to 200 times thecritical value. Merge r trees were then built to link each subhalo found at a given snapshot (includingthe main haloes) toauniquedescendant in the following 
main haloes) toauniquedescendant in the following snapshot. With these merger trees we can track the evolution of each halo throughout the simulation. In this work, we assume that both main haloes and subhaloes host galaxies at their centres. We will refer to th e galaxy at the center of a distinct halo as a ‚Äòcentral galaxy‚Äô, and the galaxies within subhaloes as ‚Äòsatellites‚Äô. There is thus one central galaxy within each FoF group but there may be any number of sattelites (including zero). Ab ini
 any number of sattelites (including zero). Ab initio models of galaxy formation predict that the stellar mass of a galaxy is tightly correlated with the depth of the potentia l well of the halo in which it formed. For central galaxies, the relevant mass is the virial mass of the main halo at the time of observation, but for satellites the situation is more complex: Subhaloes lose mass while orbiting in a larger system as their outer regions are tidally stripped. Stars ar e centrallyconcentrated
tidally stripped. Stars ar e centrallyconcentratedandmoretightlyboundthanthedark matter, however, so the stellar mass of a satellite galaxy changes only slightly after it enters a larger halo. Therefo re the subhalo mass at the time of observation is not a good tracer for the stellar mass of asatellite. We thus get a redshift-dependent SHM relation m(M,z) that depends on the virial mass of the main halo and redshift at the current epoch for central galaxies and on the infall mass of the subhalo and infall redshift for satell ite galaxies. The errors in- troduced in all these steps are typically of the order of 
 in all these steps are typically of the order of the uncertainties in the stellar populations synthesis analys is. Assuming that these errors are Gaussian, the shape of the SMF implies that at a given observed mass there are more galaxies with a lower true mass than galaxies with a higher true mass, i.e. more galaxies are scattered in than scattere d out. As a result the observed SMF is shallower than the true SMF, particularly at the massive end. It is important to include this eÔ¨Äect in our mo
d. It is important to include this eÔ¨Äect in our model. We do this by adding a Gaussian scatter to the stellar mass of each model galaxy. An ‚Äòobserved‚Äô stellar mass in our model is thus obtained in three steps: We Ô¨Årst compute the average mass for a given halo mass and redshift (or infall halo mass and infall redshift for subhaloes), then we add a Ô¨Åxedintrinsic scatter of œÉm(logm) = 0.15 dex to account for diÔ¨Äerent halo parameters and histories, and Ô¨Ånally we add an observational scatter to accou

To this end we employ the dark matter halo merger trees that have been extracted from the MSandMS-II. These merger trees connect haloes at any given epoch to their descendants at later epochs or to their progenitors at earlier epochs. As our redshift- dependentSHM relation determines the connection between galaxies and haloes at any epoch, we can use the connection between haloes across
h, we can use the connection between haloes across time to infer the connection between galaxies across time. By averaging over a sample of haloes with the same mass at z= 0, our model yields the average stellar mass growth of central galaxies as a function of halo mass. We apply this method for sets of halo masses between 1011M‚äôand 1014M‚äô. The reason why these massive galaxies grow very fast at early times, only grow slowly at intermediate redshifts and grow fast again at late times can be found in the two processes that contribute to the growth of galaxies. At high redshift, a massive galaxy grows eÔ¨Äectively by star formation, while at low redshift, stellar accretion leads to a fast growth. At intermediate redshift however, neither
 growth. At intermediate redshift however, neither of these processes is eÔ¨Äective, so that the galaxy grows only very slowly. In the following section we aim to constrain and separate these two processes. Note that while massive galaxies assemble their Ô¨Ånal stellar mass only late, the stars in these galaxies have formed much earlier, such that this result is not in conÔ¨Çict with the observation that the stars in massive systems are older than stars in low-mass galaxies. 4.1 Star formation and acc
s in low-mass galaxies. 4.1 Star formation and accretion histories The stellar mass of a galaxy can grow either by star forma- tion inside the galaxy (in-situ) or by the assembly of starsthat formed outside the galaxy and have been accreted later (ex-situ). In principle, both processes can contribute sim ul- taneously to the growth of a central galaxy, but in practice oneprocess will dominateovertheotheras afunction ofhalo mass and redshift. In order to separate the contributions from star forma
rder to separate the contributions from star formation and accretion, we have Ô¨Årst to deter- mine the amount of accretion of subhaloes onto the central object in the halo. Using our model, we can then populate these subhaloes with satellite galaxies and compute the ste l- lar accretion rate. Finally, the SFR can be determined from the diÔ¨Äerence between the total growth rate and the accre- tion rate. We use the same set of dark matter halo merger trees as before, i.e. we use all trees with a comm
trees as before, i.e. we use all trees with a common z= 0 virial mass of the main halo and assign a stellar mass to all haloes and subhaloes using our redshift-dependent SHM relation. For every simulation snapshot, we then record all satellite galaxies that are accreted onto the central galaxy until the time of the next snapshot. We note, that, by construction, this also includes orphan galaxies. At each snapshot we then sum the stellar mass of all recorded merging galaxies and divide this by th
ll recorded merging galaxies and divide this by the number of central galaxies in the sam- ple (which corresponds to the number of merger trees). Burkert 2003; Moster et al. 2011). For a diÔ¨Äerent value the resulting stellar accretion and star formation ra tes change accordingly (e.g. for fesc= 0 the accretion rates are 25 per cent higher). However there are two complications with this approach: First, it depends on the assumptions that are made to obtain the mock images (dust modeling, stellar populations synthesis modeling). Second, the reduction of the mock images would have to be done in the same way as for the observed data, which is non-trivial. Therefore we have decided to conduct a simpler comparison within the limits of the

The characteristic shape of the SHM relation is a consequence of several diÔ¨Äerent physical processes, that prevent the gas in the dark matter halo from cooling (or reheat cold gas) and forming stars. The contribution of each process varies for systems of diÔ¨Äerent mass. In low-mass systems, feedback from supernova-driven winds can be very eÔ¨Äective as winds can easily expel large amounts of gas from haloes with low esca
pel large amounts of gas from haloes with low escape velocities (e.g. Larson 1974; Dekel & Silk 1986; Oppenheimer & Dav¬¥ e 2008; Puchwein & Springel 2012). Furthermore the amount of cooled gas is reduced by the presence of a UV and an X-ray background (Navarro & Steinmetz 1997; Gnedin 2000; Benson et al. 2002; Hambrick et al. 2011) and by cos- mic rays (Jubelgas et al. 2008; Wadepuhl & Springel 2011). These eÔ¨Äects have diÔ¨Äerent eÔ¨Éciencies for diÔ¨Äerent halo masses, such that the low-mass slope of
erent halo masses, such that the low-mass slope of the SHM relation can change at the halo mass, where the eÔ¨Éciencies of the two processes cross. However, with the current data it is very hard to Ô¨Ånd this transition mass, as the error estimates are even larger than the possible variations in the slope. In massive haloes the star formation eÔ¨Éciency may be lowered by feedback from active galactic nuclei (AGN Springel et al. 2005; Croton et al. 2006; Bower et al. 2006; Somerville et al. 2008; Hambr
; Bower et al. 2006; Somerville et al. 2008; Hambrick et al. 2011; Teyssier et al. 2011;Martizzi et al.2012;Puchwein & Springel2012)which can heat the gas in the halo and prevent it from cool- ing. Another eÔ¨Äect that may be important for massive galaxies is gravitational heating (Khochfar & Ostriker 200 8; Johansson et al. 2009). In hydrodynamical zoom-in simu- lations this eÔ¨Äect is naturally included and results in a trend of lower galaxy formation eÔ¨Éciencies for more mas- sive haloes, although
ion eÔ¨Éciencies for more mas- sive haloes, although still at levels which are signiÔ¨Åcantl y too high (Oser et al. 2010; Hirschmann et al. 2012). In this framework, thecharacteristic halomass forwhichgalaxyfo r- mation is most eÔ¨Écient is the mass where the combination of the various physical processes that reduce the eÔ¨Éciencyis minimal. For massive galaxies the amount of in-situ stars is aÔ¨Äected largely by processes that are eÔ¨Äective for more massive systems (AGN feedback and gravitational heating
ve systems (AGN feedback and gravitational heating), while the amount of ex-situ stars is strongly aÔ¨Äected by pro- cesses that are eÔ¨Äective for systems of lower mass (such as SN feedback and an ionizing background radiation). Although the only observational constraints of our model are the observed SMFs from z= 0 to 4, our predic- tions for the evolution of the cosmic SFR density are in very good agreement with direct observation. This indicates tha t the evolution of the SMFs is consistent with
tha t the evolution of the SMFs is consistent with the evolution of the cosmic SFR density, although the integrated observed SFR density was previously found to be higher than the ob- served stellar mass density (e.g. Hopkins & Beacom 2006). There are two eÔ¨Äects that help to reconcile this tension. First, we employ the recent measurements by S12, who Ô¨Ånd a considerably steeper low-mass slope for the SMFs. This results in a larger amount of stellar mass in the low-mass end and thus increases the 
r mass in the low-mass end and thus increases the observed stellar mass density. Second, we include the eÔ¨Äects of observational mass error in our model. As a consequence we Ô¨Ånd an evolution in the high-mass end of the ‚Äòtrue‚Äô SMFs, especially at low red- shift. When both eÔ¨Äects are taken into account, the tension between the evolution of the stellar mass density and the integrated SFR density is considerably lowered. 6 SUMMARY In this paper, we present a galaxy evolution model that as- sumes a ti
esent a galaxy evolution model that as- sumes a tight relation between the stellar mass of a galaxy and the mass of the dark matter halo in which it resides. With this approach we populate haloes with galaxies over the entire cosmic period from z‚àº4 to the present, using the N-body Millennium simulations and observed SMFs. Unlike theclassical abundancematchingtechniqueour newmethod is self-consistent, taking into account that subhaloes are ac- creted at earlier times and with higher masses than w
ted at earlier times and with higher masses than when they are observed. We employ a redshift-dependent param- eterization of the stellar-to-halo mass relation, and popu - late haloes and subhaloes with galaxies requiring that the observed SMFs at diÔ¨Äerent redshifts be reproduced simulta- neously. We show that when observational mass errors are not taken into account, no consistent stellar mass evolution of massive galaxies can be obtained, as the central galaxy of a typical halo of mass Mvir= 1
e central galaxy of a typical halo of mass Mvir= 1014M‚äôwould lose ‚àº25 per cent of its stellar mass from z= 1 to 0. When we include observational mass errors, this tension vanishes and massi ve galaxies also have a physically consistent stellar mass evo - lution. We Ô¨Ånd that the halo mass where galaxy formation is most eÔ¨Écient is log( Mvir/M‚äô)‚àº11.8 atz= 0 and in- creases with increasing redshift to log( Mvir/M‚äô)‚àº12.5 at z= 4. Thus at high redshift, the peak eÔ¨Éciency of convert- ing baryons into s
, the peak eÔ¨Éciency of convert- ing baryons into stars occurs in more massive haloes. The normalization of the SHM ratio decreases with increasing redshift: While at z= 0 the most eÔ¨Écient haloes have con- verted‚àº23 per cent of their baryons into stars, at z= 4 the peak eÔ¨Éciency is less than ‚àº10 per cent. We further compare our results to those of hydrody- namical zoom-in simulations and semi-analytic models and Ô¨Ånd that most of the newer models show good agreement 18B. P. Moster et al. with the 
how good agreement 18B. P. Moster et al. with the eÔ¨Éciencies needed to reproduce the local observed SMFs. However, almost all have eÔ¨Éciencies which are far too high at earlier epochs, indicating that they form their stars too early. At late times star formation is quenched, so that most of the stellar mass today is already present at high redshift. In the semi-analytic models the normalization an d the slopes of the SHM relation are in good agreement with ours, but the halo mass where the eÔ¨Écien

We then combine our relation with merger trees ex- tracted from the simulations in order to predict the mean assembly histories of the stellar mass components within dark matter haloes (in situ and ex situ formation). We then go further and identify the progenitors of a given halo at earlier epochs and derive the amount of stel- lar mass that has been accreted onto the central galaxy. We Ô¨Ånd that the star formation history of central galaxies peaks at a certain redshift which depends on halo mass: for massive systems the peak occurs at high redshift while for low-mass systems it has not yet been reached by z= 0. Af- ter the peak has been reached, the SFR quickly drops, such that massive galaxies are quenched at z‚àº2. On the other hand, the stellar accretion rates increase with time for all systems.
